Part 1 Implementation
Kubernetes The Hard Way on AWS EC2 (Part 1)
Cluster Bootstrap, Networking Setup, and PKI Initialization
Overview
This document covers the initial setup of a Kubernetes cluster following the philosophy of Kubernetes The Hard Way, adapted for AWS EC2 instances. Unlike the original guide, this setup uses:
* AWS EC2 instances.
* The default admin user instead of root.
* A pre-existing AWS .pem keypair instead of generating and distributing SSH keys.
* Worker nodes named node-1 and node-2.
________________


1. Infrastructure Setup
EC2 Instances Created
Provision four Debian-based EC2 instances:
Name
	Purpose
	jumpbox
	Administration host
	server
	Kubernetes control plane
	node-1
	Worker node
	node-2
	Worker node
	Recommended minimum configuration:
* Debian 12 (Bookworm)
* 1 vCPU (2 GB RAM for server and workers)
* Same VPC and subnet (or at least private connectivity between all nodes)
________________


2. Jumpbox Setup
SSH into the jumpbox:
ssh -i <aws-key>.pem admin@<jumpbox-public-ip>


Install required utilities:
sudo apt-get update
sudo apt-get -y install wget curl vim openssl git


Clone the Kubernetes The Hard Way repository:
git clone --depth 1 https://github.com/kelseyhightower/kubernetes-the-hard-way.git
cd kubernetes-the-hard-way


Download Kubernetes component binaries:
wget -q --show-progress \
  --https-only \
  --timestamping \
  -P downloads \
  -i downloads-$(dpkg --print-architecture).txt


Extract the downloaded binaries:
{
  ARCH=$(dpkg --print-architecture)
  mkdir -p downloads/{client,cni-plugins,controller,worker}


  tar -xvf downloads/crictl-v1.32.0-linux-${ARCH}.tar.gz \
    -C downloads/worker/


  tar -xvf downloads/containerd-2.1.0-beta.0-linux-${ARCH}.tar.gz \
    --strip-components 1 \
    -C downloads/worker/


  tar -xvf downloads/cni-plugins-linux-${ARCH}-v1.6.2.tgz \
    -C downloads/cni-plugins/


  tar -xvf downloads/etcd-v3.6.0-rc.3-linux-${ARCH}.tar.gz \
    -C downloads/ \
    --strip-components 1 \
    etcd-v3.6.0-rc.3-linux-${ARCH}/etcdctl \
    etcd-v3.6.0-rc.3-linux-${ARCH}/etcd


  mv downloads/{etcdctl,kubectl} downloads/client/


  mv downloads/{etcd,kube-apiserver,kube-controller-manager,kube-scheduler} \
    downloads/controller/


  mv downloads/{kubelet,kube-proxy} downloads/worker/


  mv downloads/runc.${ARCH} downloads/worker/runc
}


rm -rf downloads/*gz


chmod +x downloads/{client,cni-plugins,controller,worker}/*


Install kubectl:
sudo cp downloads/client/kubectl /usr/local/bin/
kubectl version --client


________________


3. Machine Database
Create a file called machines.txt:
<server-private-ip> server.kubernetes.local server
<node1-private-ip>  node-1.kubernetes.local node-1 10.200.0.0/24
<node2-private-ip>  node-2.kubernetes.local node-2 10.200.1.0/24


The fourth column (POD_SUBNET) is used later when assigning Pod CIDR ranges to worker nodes.
________________


4. SSH Configuration
Instead of generating new SSH keys (ssh-keygen and ssh-copy-id), reuse the AWS keypair already attached to all EC2 instances.
Copy the PEM key to the jumpbox:
scp -i <aws-key>.pem <aws-key>.pem admin@<jumpbox-public-ip>:~/
chmod 400 ~/ani-key.pem


Create ~/.ssh/config:
Host server
    HostName <server-private-ip>
    User admin
    IdentityFile ~/.ssh/ani-key.pem


Host node-1
    HostName <node1-private-ip>
    User admin
    IdentityFile ~/.ssh/ani-key.pem


Host node-2
    HostName <node2-private-ip>
    User admin
    IdentityFile ~/.ssh/ani-key.pem


Set permissions:
chmod 600 ~/.ssh/config


Verify connectivity:
ssh server
ssh node-1
ssh node-2


________________


5. Configure Hostnames
Set the hostname of each machine using the jumpbox.
while read IP FQDN HOST SUBNET; do
    CMD="sudo sed -i 's/^127.0.1.1.*/127.0.1.1\t${FQDN} ${HOST}/' /etc/hosts"
    ssh -n ${HOST} "$CMD"
    ssh -n ${HOST} "sudo hostnamectl set-hostname ${HOST}"
    ssh -n ${HOST} "sudo systemctl restart systemd-hostnamed"
done < machines.txt


Verify:
while read IP FQDN HOST SUBNET; do
  ssh -n ${HOST} "hostname"
done < machines.txt


Expected:
server
node-1
node-2


________________


6. Configure Hostname Resolution
Create a common hosts file:
echo "" > hosts
echo "# Kubernetes The Hard Way" >> hosts


while read IP FQDN HOST SUBNET; do
    ENTRY="${IP} ${FQDN} ${HOST}"
    echo "$ENTRY" >> hosts
done < machines.txt


Append to the jumpbox:
sudo bash -c "cat hosts >> /etc/hosts"


Distribute to all cluster nodes:
while read IP FQDN HOST SUBNET; do
  scp hosts ${HOST}:~/
done < machines.txt


while read IP FQDN HOST SUBNET; do
  ssh -n ${HOST} "cat /home/admin/hosts | sudo tee -a /etc/hosts > /dev/null"
done < machines.txt


Verify hostname resolution:
ssh server
ssh node-1
ssh node-2


________________


7. PKI and Certificate Authority
Purpose
Kubernetes components authenticate each other using TLS certificates. A private Certificate Authority (CA) is created to issue identities for:
* Admin user.
* Worker nodes.
* API Server.
* Controller Manager.
* Scheduler.
* Kube Proxy.
* Service Account token signing.
The CA consists of:
* ca.key → Private signing key.
* ca.crt → Public root certificate.
________________


8. Update ca.conf
Modify the original ca.conf to reflect the worker node names:
* Replace node-0 with node-2.
* Keep node-1 unchanged.
This ensures the generated certificates match the actual cluster node names.
The worker node identities become:
* system:node:node-1
* system:node:node-2
________________


9. Generate the Certificate Authority
{
  openssl genrsa -out ca.key 4096


  openssl req -x509 -new -sha512 -noenc \
    -key ca.key \
    -days 3653 \
    -config ca.conf \
    -out ca.crt
}


________________


10. Generate Component Certificates
Define the list of identities:
certs=(
  "admin"
  "node-1"
  "node-2"
  "kube-proxy"
  "kube-scheduler"
  "kube-controller-manager"
  "kube-api-server"
  "service-accounts"
)


Generate keys, CSRs, and signed certificates:
for i in ${certs[*]}; do
  openssl genrsa -out "${i}.key" 4096


  openssl req -new \
    -key "${i}.key" \
    -sha256 \
    -config "ca.conf" \
    -section ${i} \
    -out "${i}.csr"


  openssl x509 -req \
    -days 3653 \
    -in "${i}.csr" \
    -copy_extensions copyall \
    -sha256 \
    -CA "ca.crt" \
    -CAkey "ca.key" \
    -CAcreateserial \
    -out "${i}.crt"
done


Verify:
ls -1 *.crt *.key *.csr


________________


11. Distribute Worker Node Certificates
Create the kubelet directory, copy the files to the node, then move them into the final location:
for host in node-1 node-2; do
  ssh ${host} "sudo mkdir -p /var/lib/kubelet"


  scp ca.crt ${host}:~/ca.crt
  scp ${host}.crt ${host}:~/kubelet.crt
  scp ${host}.key ${host}:~/kubelet.key


  ssh ${host} "
    sudo mv ~/ca.crt /var/lib/kubelet/ca.crt &&
    sudo mv ~/kubelet.crt /var/lib/kubelet/kubelet.crt &&
    sudo mv ~/kubelet.key /var/lib/kubelet/kubelet.key
  "
done


Verify:
for host in node-1 node-2; do
  ssh ${host} "sudo ls -l /var/lib/kubelet"
done


________________


12. Distribute Control Plane Certificates
Copy the required certificates to the control plane node:
scp \
  ca.key \
  ca.crt \
  kube-api-server.key \
  kube-api-server.crt \
  service-accounts.key \
  service-accounts.crt \
  server:~/


________________


13. Summary
At this stage:
* Four EC2 instances are provisioned.
* SSH access between nodes is configured.
* Hostnames and local name resolution are configured.
* Kubernetes binaries are downloaded.
* A private Certificate Authority has been created.
* TLS certificates have been generated for all Kubernetes components.
* Worker node certificates have been distributed.
* Control plane certificates have been copied to the server node.
The cluster now has the cryptographic identity infrastructure required to begin configuring authentication and bringing up the Kubernetes control plane.
Next: Generate Kubernetes configuration (kubeconfig) files for component authentication.


Part 3 implementation
# Kubernetes The Hard Way on AWS EC2
# Part 3: Encryption Config and Bootstrapping etcd


## Overview


In this section, we:
1. Generate a Kubernetes encryption key.
2. Create the encryption configuration file.
3. Copy it to the control plane node.
4. Install and bootstrap a single-node etcd cluster.
5. Verify that etcd is running correctly.


---


## 1. Generate the Data Encryption Key


Generate a random 32-byte key and Base64 encode it:


```bash
export ENCRYPTION_KEY=$(head -c 32 /dev/urandom | base64)
```


Verify (optional):


```bash
echo $ENCRYPTION_KEY
```


This key will later be used by the Kubernetes API Server to encrypt Kubernetes Secrets before storing them in etcd.


---


## 2. Generate the Encryption Configuration File


Create the encryption configuration YAML using the template provided by the repository:


```bash
envsubst < configs/encryption-config.yaml > encryption-config.yaml
```


This substitutes the `ENCRYPTION_KEY` environment variable into the template.


---


## 3. Copy Encryption Config to the Control Plane Node


Since this setup uses the `admin` user instead of `root`, the command is modified as follows:


```bash
scp encryption-config.yaml admin@server:~/
```


---


# Bootstrapping etcd


## 4. Copy Required Files to the Control Plane


From the jumpbox:


```bash
scp \
  downloads/controller/etcd \
  downloads/client/etcdctl \
  units/etcd.service \
  admin@server:~/
```


The following files are copied:
- `etcd` (database server binary)
- `etcdctl` (CLI utility)
- `etcd.service` (systemd unit file)


---


## 5. Login to the Server


```bash
ssh server
```


---


## 6. Install the etcd Binaries


Move the binaries into `/usr/local/bin`:


```bash
sudo mv etcd etcdctl /usr/local/bin/
```


This makes them globally executable.


---


## 7. Configure etcd Directories


Create the configuration and data directories:


```bash
{
  sudo mkdir -p /etc/etcd /var/lib/etcd
  sudo chmod 700 /var/lib/etcd
  sudo cp ca.crt kube-api-server.key kube-api-server.crt \
    /etc/etcd/
}
```


Notes:
- `/etc/etcd` stores certificates and configuration.
- `/var/lib/etcd` stores the persistent database.
- `chmod 700` restricts access to the etcd data directory.
- The API Server certificate and key are copied because they will later be used by the API Server to authenticate to etcd.


---


## 8. Install the systemd Service


Install the etcd service definition:


```bash
sudo mv etcd.service /etc/systemd/system/
```


---


## 9. Start the etcd Service


Reload systemd, enable automatic startup, and start etcd:


```bash
{
  sudo systemctl daemon-reload
  sudo systemctl enable etcd
  sudo systemctl start etcd
}
```


Explanation:
- `daemon-reload` → Reloads all systemd unit definitions.
- `enable` → Starts etcd automatically after every reboot.
- `start` → Starts the etcd process immediately.


---


## 10. Verify etcd


Check the etcd cluster membership:


```bash
etcdctl member list
```


Expected output:


```text
6702b0a34e2cfd39, started, controller, http://127.0.0.1:2380, http://127.0.0.1:2379, false
```


Meaning:
- Member ID: `6702b0a34e2cfd39`
- Status: `started`
- Name: `controller`
- Peer URL: `http://127.0.0.1:2380`
- Client URL: `http://127.0.0.1:2379`
- Learner Mode: `false`


---


## AWS-Specific Modifications


| Original Tutorial | AWS EC2 Version |
|-------------------|-----------------|
| `root@server` | `admin@server` |
| `mv ... /usr/local/bin/` | `sudo mv ... /usr/local/bin/` |
| `mkdir /etc/etcd` | `sudo mkdir /etc/etcd` |
| `chmod 700 /var/lib/etcd` | `sudo chmod 700 /var/lib/etcd` |
| `cp ... /etc/etcd/` | `sudo cp ... /etc/etcd/` |
| `mv etcd.service /etc/systemd/system/` | `sudo mv etcd.service /etc/systemd/system/` |
| `systemctl ...` | `sudo systemctl ...` |


---


## Current Cluster State


At the end of Part 3:


- Jumpbox is configured.
- Certificates and kubeconfigs have been generated.
- Encryption configuration has been created.
- Single-node etcd cluster is installed and running.
- etcd is managed by systemd.
- Kubernetes control plane components have not yet been started.


The cluster currently consists of an operational etcd database waiting for the API Server to connect.


Part 3 deep dive
# Kubernetes The Hard Way
# Part 3 Deep Dive: Encryption, etcd, and Systemd Internals


## 1. Why etcd Exists


Kubernetes components are intentionally stateless.


Components like:
- kube-apiserver,
- kube-scheduler,
- kube-controller-manager,


do not permanently store cluster information themselves. Instead, they store and retrieve all cluster state from etcd.


Conceptually:


kubectl
↓
kube-apiserver
↓
etcd


If the API Server crashes and restarts, nothing is lost because all state remains stored in etcd.


---


## 2. What is etcd?


etcd is a:
- Distributed
- Strongly consistent
- Fault tolerant
- Key-value database


written in Go and built around the Raft consensus algorithm.


Conceptually it behaves like a giant distributed dictionary:


```
/registry/pods/default/nginx
/registry/deployments/default/frontend
/registry/secrets/default/db-password
```


Each key maps to a serialized Kubernetes object.


---


## 3. How Kubernetes Objects are Stored


When a user runs:


```bash
kubectl apply -f deployment.yaml
```


the flow is:


1. kubectl parses YAML.
2. YAML is converted into JSON.
3. JSON is sent to the API Server over HTTPS.
4. API Server validates the request.
5. API Server serializes the internal object.
6. The object is written into etcd.


Important:
- etcd does NOT store the original YAML file.
- etcd stores the Kubernetes internal object representation.


---


## 4. etcd Key Hierarchy


Objects are stored using key prefixes.


Examples:


```
/registry/pods/default/nginx
/registry/deployments/default/frontend
/registry/services/default/kubernetes
/registry/secrets/default/db-password
```


Think of this as a logical folder hierarchy.


---


## 5. Why Components Don't Talk Directly to etcd


Only the API Server should directly interact with etcd.


This provides:
- Authentication.
- Authorization (RBAC).
- Validation.
- Admission Controllers.
- Auditing.


Architecture:


                kube-apiserver
                      |
     +----------------+----------------+
     |                |                |
 kubectl         scheduler        kubelet
     |                |                |
     +----------------+----------------+
                      |
                     etcd


---


## 6. Watches and the Event Model


etcd supports "watch" operations.


Controllers do not repeatedly poll for changes.


Instead:


1. API Server writes object into etcd.
2. etcd generates a watch event.
3. API Server forwards the event.
4. Scheduler / Controllers react immediately.


This event-driven model is the foundation of Kubernetes reconciliation loops.


---


## 7. etcd Cluster Architecture


Production etcd clusters usually have 3 or 5 members.


Example:


          Leader
          etcd-1
         /      \
        /        \
   etcd-2      etcd-3


The nodes synchronize using the Raft consensus algorithm.


One node becomes the Leader.
Followers replicate writes from the Leader.
A majority (quorum) must acknowledge a write before it is committed.


---


## 8. Single Node etcd in Kubernetes The Hard Way


This lab simplifies the architecture:


+---------------------------+
|       server EC2          |
|---------------------------|
| kube-apiserver (later)    |
| etcd                      |
+---------------------------+


No replication occurs because only one member exists.


---


## 9. Kubernetes Secrets Encryption


Secrets are stored in etcd like any other object.


Without encryption-at-rest:


Secret
↓
API Server
↓
Plaintext stored in etcd


With encryption-at-rest:


Secret
↓
API Server
↓
Encrypt using AES key
↓
Encrypted bytes stored in etcd


The encryption key is generated using:


```bash
head -c 32 /dev/urandom | base64
```


The encryption configuration file tells the API Server how to perform this encryption.


---


## 10. Why the API Server Certificate is Copied to /etc/etcd


This often causes confusion.


The certificate is NOT copied because etcd becomes the API Server.


Instead:
- etcd acts as the TLS server.
- kube-apiserver acts as the TLS client.


Later, the API Server starts with flags similar to:


```
--etcd-servers=https://127.0.0.1:2379
--etcd-cafile=/etc/etcd/ca.crt
--etcd-certfile=/etc/etcd/kube-api-server.crt
--etcd-keyfile=/etc/etcd/kube-api-server.key
```


Thus, the API Server loads its client certificate from `/etc/etcd`.


The directory simply acts as a convenient storage location because both processes run on the same machine.


---


## 11. systemd


systemd is Linux's service manager.


Very rough analogy:


| Kubernetes | Linux |
|------------|--------|
| Deployment YAML | systemd service |
| kubelet | systemd |
| Pod | Linux process |
| kubectl apply | systemctl start |


A systemd service file tells Linux:
- what binary to execute,
- which arguments to pass,
- restart policy,
- startup dependencies.


---


## 12. Understanding etcd.service


The file `/etc/systemd/system/etcd.service` is a blueprint for starting the etcd process.


### Type=notify


etcd explicitly tells systemd when it has fully initialized and is ready to accept requests.


### ExecStart


Runs:


```
/usr/local/bin/etcd
```


with all the specified flags.


### --name


Logical identifier of this etcd member.


### --listen-client-urls


Port where clients connect.


Default:
- 2379


Clients include:
- kube-apiserver,
- etcdctl,
- backup utilities.


### --listen-peer-urls


Port used for etcd-to-etcd communication.


Default:
- 2380


Used for:
- Raft replication,
- Leader election.


### --advertise-client-urls


Address this node tells clients to use.


### --initial-cluster


Defines the initial cluster membership.


Single node example:


```
controller=http://127.0.0.1:2380
```


### --initial-cluster-token


Unique identifier used when bootstrapping a new cluster.


### --initial-cluster-state=new


Indicates this is a newly created cluster.


### --data-dir


Persistent storage location for the etcd database.


```
/var/lib/etcd/
├── member/
│   ├── snap/
│   └── wal/
```


---


## 13. Write Ahead Log (WAL)


Before modifying the database, etcd first appends the operation to the WAL.


Example:


```
1. Create namespace.
2. Create Deployment.
3. Create Pod.
4. Update Pod status.
```


If the machine crashes, etcd can replay the WAL and recover safely.


This concept is common across enterprise databases.


---


## 14. systemctl Commands


### daemon-reload


Reloads systemd's internal cache after adding or modifying service files.


### enable


Creates symbolic links so the service automatically starts after reboot.


Example:


```
/etc/systemd/system/multi-user.target.wants/etcd.service
→ /etc/systemd/system/etcd.service
```


### start


Launches the etcd process immediately.


Startup sequence:


systemd
↓
Launch etcd binary
↓
Open ports 2379 and 2380
↓
Initialize database
↓
Initialize Raft
↓
Signal READY=1
↓
Wait for client requests


---


## 15. etcdctl member list


Command:


```bash
etcdctl member list
```


This queries the etcd server for cluster membership information.


Example output:


```
6702b0a34e2cfd39, started, controller, http://127.0.0.1:2380, http://127.0.0.1:2379, false
```


Fields:


- `6702b0a34e2cfd39` → Unique member ID.
- `started` → Current health state.
- `controller` → Member name.
- `127.0.0.1:2380` → Peer communication endpoint.
- `127.0.0.1:2379` → Client API endpoint.
- `false` → Not a learner node (full voting member).


---


## 16. Network Flow of etcdctl


When running:


```bash
etcdctl member list
```


the following occurs:


etcdctl
↓
TCP SYN
↓
TCP SYN-ACK
↓
TCP ACK
↓
HTTP/gRPC request
↓
etcd returns membership data
↓
TCP FIN


Because both processes run on the same host, communication happens entirely over the loopback interface (`127.0.0.1`).


---


## 17. Current Mental Model


At the end of Part 3, the cluster architecture is:


                   +----------------------+
                   |      Jumpbox         |
                   +----------+-----------+
                              |
                              | SSH
                              |
                +-------------v-------------+
                |       server EC2          |
                |---------------------------|
                | etcd                      |
                | - Client Port: 2379       |
                | - Peer Port: 2380         |
                | - Data: /var/lib/etcd     |
                |                           |
                | (API Server not started)  |
                +---------------------------+


At this point, you have successfully built the persistent storage engine that will become the single source of truth for the entire Kubernetes control plane.

admin@ip-172-31-16-36:~/kubernetes-the-hard-way$ {
  ARCH=$(dpkg --print-architecture)
  mkdir -p downloads/{client,cni-plugins,controller,worker}
  tar -xvf downloads/crictl-v1.32.0-linux-${ARCH}.tar.gz \
    -C downloads/worker/
  tar -xvf downloads/containerd-2.1.0-beta.0-linux-${ARCH}.tar.gz \
    --strip-components 1 \
    -C downloads/worker/
  tar -xvf downloads/cni-plugins-linux-${ARCH}-v1.6.2.tgz \
    -C downloads/cni-plugins/
  tar -xvf downloads/etcd-v3.6.0-rc.3-linux-${ARCH}.tar.gz \
    -C downloads/ \
    --strip-components 1 \
    etcd-v3.6.0-rc.3-linux-${ARCH}/etcdctl \
    etcd-v3.6.0-rc.3-linux-${ARCH}/etcd
  mv downloads/{etcdctl,kubectl} downloads/client/
  mv downloads/{etcd,kube-apiserver,kube-controller-manager,kube-scheduler} \
    downloads/controller/
  mv downloads/{kubelet,kube-proxy} downloads/worker/
  mv downloads/runc.${ARCH} downloads/worker/runc
}

[req]
distinguished_name = req_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
x509_extensions    = ca_x509_extensions


[ca_x509_extensions]
basicConstraints = CA:TRUE
keyUsage         = cRLSign, keyCertSign


[req_distinguished_name]
C   = US
ST  = Washington
L   = Seattle
CN  = CA


[admin]
distinguished_name = admin_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = default_req_extensions


[admin_distinguished_name]
CN = admin
O  = system:masters


# Service Accounts
#
# The Kubernetes Controller Manager leverages a key pair to generate
# and sign service account tokens as described in the
# [managing service accounts](https://kubernetes.io/docs/admin/service-accounts-admin/)
# documentation.


[service-accounts]
distinguished_name = service-accounts_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = default_req_extensions


[service-accounts_distinguished_name]
CN = service-accounts


# Worker Nodes
#
# Kubernetes uses a [special-purpose authorization mode](https://kubernetes.io/docs/admin/authorization/node/)
# called Node Authorizer, that specifically authorizes API requests made
# by [Kubelets](https://kubernetes.io/docs/concepts/overview/components/#kubelet).
# In order to be authorized by the Node Authorizer, Kubelets must use a credential
# that identifies them as being in the `system:nodes` group, with a username
# of `system:node:<nodeName>`.


[node-0]
distinguished_name = node-0_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = node-0_req_extensions


[node-0_req_extensions]
basicConstraints     = CA:FALSE
extendedKeyUsage     = clientAuth, serverAuth
keyUsage             = critical, digitalSignature, keyEncipherment
nsCertType           = client
nsComment            = "Node-0 Certificate"
subjectAltName       = DNS:node-0, IP:127.0.0.1
subjectKeyIdentifier = hash


[node-0_distinguished_name]
CN = system:node:node-0
O  = system:nodes
C  = US
ST = Washington
L  = Seattle


[node-1]
distinguished_name = node-1_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = node-1_req_extensions


[node-1_req_extensions]
basicConstraints     = CA:FALSE
extendedKeyUsage     = clientAuth, serverAuth
keyUsage             = critical, digitalSignature, keyEncipherment
nsCertType           = client
nsComment            = "Node-1 Certificate"
subjectAltName       = DNS:node-1, IP:127.0.0.1
subjectKeyIdentifier = hash


[node-1_distinguished_name]
CN = system:node:node-1
O  = system:nodes
C  = US
ST = Washington
L  = Seattle

# API Server
#
# The Kubernetes API server is automatically assigned the `kubernetes`
# internal dns name, which will be linked to the first IP address (`10.32.0.1`)
# from the address range (`10.32.0.0/24`) reserved for internal cluster
# services.


[kube-api-server]
distinguished_name = kube-api-server_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = kube-api-server_req_extensions


[kube-api-server_req_extensions]
basicConstraints     = CA:FALSE
extendedKeyUsage     = clientAuth, serverAuth
keyUsage             = critical, digitalSignature, keyEncipherment
nsCertType           = client, server
nsComment            = "Kube API Server Certificate"
subjectAltName       = @kube-api-server_alt_names
subjectKeyIdentifier = hash


[kube-api-server_alt_names]
IP.0  = 127.0.0.1
IP.1  = 10.32.0.1
DNS.0 = kubernetes
DNS.1 = kubernetes.default
DNS.2 = kubernetes.default.svc
DNS.3 = kubernetes.default.svc.cluster
DNS.4 = kubernetes.svc.cluster.local
DNS.5 = server.kubernetes.local

admin@ip-172-31-16-36:~/kubernetes-the-hard-way$ for i in ${certs[*]}; do
  openssl genrsa -out "${i}.key" 4096


  openssl req -new -key "${i}.key" -sha256 \
    -config "ca.conf" -section ${i} \
    -out "${i}.csr"


  openssl x509 -req -days 3653 -in "${i}.csr" \
    -copy_extensions copyall \
    -sha256 -CA "ca.crt" \
    -CAkey "ca.key" \
    -CAcreateserial \
    -out "${i}.crt"
done

[req]
distinguished_name = req_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
x509_extensions    = ca_x509_extensions


[ca_x509_extensions]
basicConstraints = CA:TRUE
keyUsage         = cRLSign, keyCertSign


[req_distinguished_name]
C   = US
ST  = Washington
L   = Seattle
CN  = CA


[admin]
distinguished_name = admin_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = default_req_extensions


[admin_distinguished_name]
CN = admin
O  = system:masters


# Service Accounts
#
# The Kubernetes Controller Manager leverages a key pair to generate
# and sign service account tokens as described in the
# [managing service accounts](https://kubernetes.io/docs/admin/service-accounts-admin/)
# documentation.


[service-accounts]
distinguished_name = service-accounts_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = default_req_extensions


[service-accounts_distinguished_name]
CN = service-accounts


# Worker Nodes
#
# Kubernetes uses a [special-purpose authorization mode](https://kubernetes.io/docs/admin/authorization/node/)
# called Node Authorizer, that specifically authorizes API requests made
# by [Kubelets](https://kubernetes.io/docs/concepts/overview/components/#kubelet).
# In order to be authorized by the Node Authorizer, Kubelets must use a credential
# that identifies them as being in the `system:nodes` group, with a username
# of `system:node:<nodeName>`.


[node-1]
distinguished_name = node-1_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = node-1_req_extensions


[node-1_req_extensions]
basicConstraints     = CA:FALSE
extendedKeyUsage     = clientAuth, serverAuth
keyUsage             = critical, digitalSignature, keyEncipherment
nsCertType           = client
nsComment            = "Node-1 Certificate"
subjectAltName       = DNS:node-1, IP:127.0.0.1
subjectKeyIdentifier = hash


[node-1_distinguished_name]
CN = system:node:node-1
O  = system:nodes
C  = US
ST = Washington
L  = Seattle


[node-2]
distinguished_name = node-2_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = node-2_req_extensions


[node-2_req_extensions]
basicConstraints     = CA:FALSE
extendedKeyUsage     = clientAuth, serverAuth
keyUsage             = critical, digitalSignature, keyEncipherment
nsCertType           = client
nsComment            = "Node-2 Certificate"
subjectAltName       = DNS:node-2, IP:127.0.0.1
subjectKeyIdentifier = hash


[node-2_distinguished_name]
CN = system:node:node-2
O  = system:nodes
C  = US
ST = Washington
L  = Seattle

# Controller Manager

# API Server
#
# The Kubernetes API server is automatically assigned the `kubernetes`
# internal dns name, which will be linked to the first IP address (`10.32.0.1`)
# from the address range (`10.32.0.0/24`) reserved for internal cluster
# services.


[kube-api-server]
distinguished_name = kube-api-server_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = kube-api-server_req_extensions


[kube-api-server_req_extensions]
basicConstraints     = CA:FALSE
extendedKeyUsage     = clientAuth, serverAuth
keyUsage             = critical, digitalSignature, keyEncipherment
nsCertType           = client, server
nsComment            = "Kube API Server Certificate"
subjectAltName       = @kube-api-server_alt_names
subjectKeyIdentifier = hash


[kube-api-server_alt_names]
IP.0  = 127.0.0.1
IP.1  = 10.32.0.1
DNS.0 = kubernetes
DNS.1 = kubernetes.default
DNS.2 = kubernetes.default.svc
DNS.3 = kubernetes.default.svc.cluster
DNS.4 = kubernetes.svc.cluster.local
DNS.5 = server.kubernetes.local
DNS.6 = api-server.kubernetes.local


[kube-api-server_distinguished_name]
CN = kubernetes
C  = US
ST = Washington
L  = Seattle




[default_req_extensions]

extendedKeyUsage     = clientAuth
keyUsage             = critical, digitalSignature, keyEncipherment
nsCertType           = client
nsComment            = "Admin Client Certificate"
subjectKeyIdentifier = hash

    -config "ca.conf" -section ${i} \
    -out "${i}.csr"


  openssl x509 -req -days 3653 -in "${i}.csr" \
    -copy_extensions copyall \
    -sha256 -CA "ca.crt" \
    -CAkey "ca.key" \
    -CAcreateserial \
    -out "${i}.crt"
done

[req]
distinguished_name = req_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
x509_extensions    = ca_x509_extensions

basicConstraints = CA:TRUE
keyUsage         = cRLSign, keyCertSign


[req_distinguished_name]
C   = US
ST  = Washington
L   = Seattle
CN  = CA


[admin]
distinguished_name = admin_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = default_req_extensions


[admin_distinguished_name]
CN = admin
O  = system:masters


# Service Accounts
#
# The Kubernetes Controller Manager leverages a key pair to generate
# and sign service account tokens as described in the
# [managing service accounts](https://kubernetes.io/docs/admin/service-accounts-admin/)
# documentation.


[service-accounts]
distinguished_name = service-accounts_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = default_req_extensions


[service-accounts_distinguished_name]
CN = service-accounts


# Worker Nodes
#
# Kubernetes uses a [special-purpose authorization mode](https://kubernetes.io/docs/admin/authorization/node/)
# called Node Authorizer, that specifically authorizes API requests made
# by [Kubelets](https://kubernetes.io/docs/concepts/overview/components/#kubelet).
# In order to be authorized by the Node Authorizer, Kubelets must use a credential
# that identifies them as being in the `system:nodes` group, with a username
# of `system:node:<nodeName>`.


[node-1]
distinguished_name = node-1_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = node-1_req_extensions


[node-1_req_extensions]
basicConstraints     = CA:FALSE
extendedKeyUsage     = clientAuth, serverAuth
keyUsage             = critical, digitalSignature, keyEncipherment
nsCertType           = client
nsComment            = "Node-1 Certificate"
subjectAltName       = DNS:node-1, IP:127.0.0.1
subjectKeyIdentifier = hash


[node-1_distinguished_name]

O  = system:nodes
C  = US

L  = Seattle

# API Server
#
# The Kubernetes API server is automatically assigned the `kubernetes`
# internal dns name, which will be linked to the first IP address (`10.32.0.1`)
# from the address range (`10.32.0.0/24`) reserved for internal cluster
# services.


[kube-api-server]
distinguished_name = kube-api-server_distinguished_name
prompt             = no
req_extensions     = kube-api-server_req_extensions


[kube-api-server_req_extensions]
basicConstraints     = CA:FALSE
extendedKeyUsage     = clientAuth, serverAuth
keyUsage             = critical, digitalSignature, keyEncipherment
nsCertType           = client, server
nsComment            = "Kube API Server Certificate"
subjectAltName       = @kube-api-server_alt_names
subjectKeyIdentifier = hash

DNS.4 = kubernetes.svc.cluster.local
DNS.5 = server.kubernetes.local
DNS.6 = api-server.kubernetes.local


[kube-api-server_distinguished_name]

C  = US
ST = Washington
L  = Seattle

  kubectl config set-cluster kubernetes-the-hard-way \
    --certificate-authority=ca.crt \
    --embed-certs=true \
    --server=https://server.kubernetes.local:6443 \
    --kubeconfig=${host}.kubeconfig


  kubectl config set-credentials system:node:${host} \
    --client-certificate=${host}.crt \
    --client-key=${host}.key \
    --embed-certs=true \
    --kubeconfig=${host}.kubeconfig


  kubectl config set-context default \
    --cluster=kubernetes-the-hard-way \
    --user=system:node:${host} \
    --kubeconfig=${host}.kubeconfig


  kubectl config use-context default \
    --kubeconfig=${host}.kubeconfig
done

NAME:
  etcdctl - A simple command line client for etcd3.


USAGE:
  etcdctl [flags]


VERSION:
  3.6.0-rc.3


API VERSION:
  3.6


COMMANDS:
  alarm disarm                  Disarms all alarms
  alarm list                    Lists all alarms
  auth disable                  Disables authentication
  auth enable                   Enables authentication
  auth status                   Returns authentication status
  check datascale               Check the memory usage of holding data for different workloads on a given server endpoint.
  check perf                    Check the performance of the etcd cluster
  compaction                    Compacts the event history in etcd
  completion                    Generate completion script
  defrag                        Defragments the storage of the etcd members with given endpoints
  del                           Removes the specified key or range of keys [key, range_end)
  downgrade cancel              Cancel the ongoing downgrade action to cluster
  downgrade enable              Start a downgrade action to cluster
  downgrade validate            Validate downgrade capability before starting downgrade
  elect                         Observes and participates in leader election
  endpoint hashkv               Prints the KV history hash for each endpoint in --endpoints
  endpoint health               Checks the healthiness of endpoints specified in `--endpoints` flag
  endpoint status               Prints out the status of endpoints specified in `--endpoints` flag
  get                           Gets the key or a range of keys
  help                          Help about any command
  lease grant                   Creates leases
  lease keep-alive              Keeps leases alive (renew)
  lease list                    List all active leases
  lease revoke                  Revokes leases
  lease timetolive              Get lease information
  lock                          Acquires a named lock
  make-mirror                   Makes a mirror at the destination etcd cluster
  member add                    Adds a member into the cluster
  member list                   Lists all members in the cluster
  member promote                Promotes a non-voting member in the cluster
  member remove                 Removes a member from the cluster
  member update                 Updates a member in the cluster
  move-leader                   Transfers leadership to another etcd cluster member.
  put                           Puts the given key into the store
  role add                      Adds a new role
  role delete                   Deletes a role
  role get                      Gets detailed information of a role
  role grant-permission         Grants a key to a role
  role list                     Lists all roles
  role revoke-permission        Revokes a key from a role
  snapshot save                 Stores an etcd node backend snapshot to a given file
  txn                           Txn processes all the requests in one transaction
  user add                      Adds a new user
  user delete                   Deletes a user
  user get                      Gets detailed information of a user
  user grant-role               Grants a role to a user
  user list                     Lists all users
  user passwd                   Changes password of user
  user revoke-role              Revokes a role from a user
  version                       Prints the version of etcdctl
  watch                         Watches events stream on keys or prefixes

NAME:
  etcdctl - A simple command line client for etcd3.


USAGE:
  etcdctl [flags]


VERSION:
  3.6.0-rc.3


API VERSION:
  3.6


COMMANDS:
  alarm disarm                  Disarms all alarms
  alarm list                    Lists all alarms
  auth disable                  Disables authentication
  auth enable                   Enables authentication
  auth status                   Returns authentication status
  check datascale               Check the memory usage of holding data for different workloads on a given server endpoint.
  check perf                    Check the performance of the etcd cluster
  compaction                    Compacts the event history in etcd
  completion                    Generate completion script
  defrag                        Defragments the storage of the etcd members with given endpoints
  del                           Removes the specified key or range of keys [key, range_end)
  downgrade cancel              Cancel the ongoing downgrade action to cluster
  downgrade enable              Start a downgrade action to cluster
  downgrade validate            Validate downgrade capability before starting downgrade
  elect                         Observes and participates in leader election
  endpoint hashkv               Prints the KV history hash for each endpoint in --endpoints
  endpoint health               Checks the healthiness of endpoints specified in `--endpoints` flag
  endpoint status               Prints out the status of endpoints specified in `--endpoints` flag
  get                           Gets the key or a range of keys
  help                          Help about any command
  lease grant                   Creates leases
  lease keep-alive              Keeps leases alive (renew)
  lease list                    List all active leases
  lease revoke                  Revokes leases
  lease timetolive              Get lease information
  lock                          Acquires a named lock
  make-mirror                   Makes a mirror at the destination etcd cluster
  member add                    Adds a member into the cluster
  member list                   Lists all members in the cluster
  member promote                Promotes a non-voting member in the cluster
  member remove                 Removes a member from the cluster
  member update                 Updates a member in the cluster
  move-leader                   Transfers leadership to another etcd cluster member.
  put                           Puts the given key into the store
  role add                      Adds a new role
  role delete                   Deletes a role
  role get                      Gets detailed information of a role
  role grant-permission         Grants a key to a role
  role list                     Lists all roles
  role revoke-permission        Revokes a key from a role
  snapshot save                 Stores an etcd node backend snapshot to a given file
  txn                           Txn processes all the requests in one transaction
  user add                      Adds a new user
  user delete                   Deletes a user
  user get                      Gets detailed information of a user
  user grant-role               Grants a role to a user
  user list                     Lists all users
  user passwd                   Changes password of user
  user revoke-role              Revokes a role from a user
  version                       Prints the version of etcdctl
  watch                         Watches events stream on keys or prefixes

[Unit]
Description=etcd
Documentation=https://github.com/etcd-io/etcd


[Service]
Type=notify
ExecStart=/usr/local/bin/etcd \
  --name controller \
  --initial-advertise-peer-urls http://127.0.0.1:2380 \
  --listen-peer-urls http://127.0.0.1:2380 \
  --listen-client-urls http://127.0.0.1:2379 \
  --advertise-client-urls http://127.0.0.1:2379 \
  --initial-cluster-token etcd-cluster-0 \
  --initial-cluster controller=http://127.0.0.1:2380 \
  --initial-cluster-state new \
  --data-dir=/var/lib/etcd
Restart=on-failure
RestartSec=5


[Install]
WantedBy=multi-user.target

Part 4 Implementation
# Kubernetes The Hard Way (AWS Edition)
# Part 4 - Bootstrapping the Kubernetes Control Plane


## Objective


In this stage, we bootstrap the Kubernetes control plane by installing and configuring:


- kube-apiserver
- kube-controller-manager
- kube-scheduler


The control plane is installed on the `server` EC2 instance.


---


## Prerequisites


### On the Jumpbox


Copy the required binaries and configuration files to the server node.


```bash
scp \
  downloads/controller/kube-apiserver \
  downloads/controller/kube-controller-manager \
  downloads/controller/kube-scheduler \
  downloads/client/kubectl \
  units/kube-apiserver.service \
  units/kube-controller-manager.service \
  units/kube-scheduler.service \
  configs/kube-scheduler.yaml \
  configs/kube-apiserver-to-kubelet.yaml \
  admin@server:~/
```


---


## Connect to the Server


```bash
ssh server
```


---


## Create Kubernetes Configuration Directory


```bash
sudo mkdir -p /etc/kubernetes/config
```


This directory stores component-specific configuration files.


---


## Install Kubernetes Controller Binaries


```bash
sudo mv \
  kube-apiserver \
  kube-controller-manager \
  kube-scheduler \
  kubectl \
  /usr/local/bin/
```


Verify:


```bash
which kube-apiserver
which kube-controller-manager
which kube-scheduler
which kubectl
```


---


## Configure the Kubernetes API Server


Create storage directory:


```bash
sudo mkdir -p /var/lib/kubernetes/
```


Move certificates and encryption config:


```bash
sudo mv \
  ca.crt \
  ca.key \
  kube-api-server.crt \
  kube-api-server.key \
  service-accounts.crt \
  service-accounts.key \
  encryption-config.yaml \
  /var/lib/kubernetes/
```


Install the systemd unit:


```bash
sudo mv kube-apiserver.service \
  /etc/systemd/system/
```


Verify:


```bash
cat /etc/systemd/system/kube-apiserver.service
```


---


## Configure the Kubernetes Controller Manager


Move the kubeconfig:


```bash
sudo mv kube-controller-manager.kubeconfig \
  /var/lib/kubernetes/
```


Install the systemd service:


```bash
sudo mv kube-controller-manager.service \
  /etc/systemd/system/
```


Verify:


```bash
cat /etc/systemd/system/kube-controller-manager.service
```


---


## Configure the Kubernetes Scheduler


Move the scheduler kubeconfig:


```bash
sudo mv kube-scheduler.kubeconfig \
  /var/lib/kubernetes/
```


Move scheduler configuration file:


```bash
sudo mv kube-scheduler.yaml \
  /etc/kubernetes/config/
```


Install scheduler systemd unit:


```bash
sudo mv kube-scheduler.service \
  /etc/systemd/system/
```


Verify:


```bash
cat /etc/kubernetes/config/kube-scheduler.yaml


cat /etc/systemd/system/kube-scheduler.service
```


---


## Start the Control Plane Components


Reload systemd:


```bash
sudo systemctl daemon-reload
```


Enable services at boot:


```bash
sudo systemctl enable \
  kube-apiserver \
  kube-controller-manager \
  kube-scheduler
```


Start services:


```bash
sudo systemctl start \
  kube-apiserver \
  kube-controller-manager \
  kube-scheduler
```


---


## Verification


Check API Server status:


```bash
sudo systemctl is-active kube-apiserver
```


Expected:


```
active
```


Detailed status:


```bash
sudo systemctl status kube-apiserver
```


View logs:


```bash
journalctl -u kube-apiserver
```


---


## Verify the Control Plane using kubectl


```bash
kubectl cluster-info \
  --kubeconfig admin.kubeconfig
```


Expected:


```
Kubernetes control plane is running at https://127.0.0.1:6443
```


---


## Configure RBAC for API Server → Kubelet Communication


Apply the ClusterRole and ClusterRoleBinding:


```bash
kubectl apply \
  -f kube-apiserver-to-kubelet.yaml \
  --kubeconfig admin.kubeconfig
```


Expected:


```
clusterrole.rbac.authorization.k8s.io/system:kube-apiserver-to-kubelet created
clusterrolebinding.rbac.authorization.k8s.io/system:kube-apiserver created
```


---


## Verify API Server Connectivity from the Jumpbox


Ensure `/etc/hosts` contains:


```
172.31.20.244 server.kubernetes.local server
172.31.19.88 node-1.kubernetes.local node-1
172.31.25.215 node-2.kubernetes.local node-2
```


Verify DNS resolution:


```bash
ping -c 1 server.kubernetes.local
```


Test API Server TLS endpoint:


```bash
curl --cacert ca.crt \
  https://server.kubernetes.local:6443/version
```


Expected output:


```json
{
  "major": "1",
  "minor": "32",
  "gitVersion": "v1.32.3",
  ...
}
```


---


## Notes


- The API Server certificate SAN must match the hostname used in the curl command.
- If custom hostnames are used, either:
  - update `/etc/hosts` to match the certificate SAN, or
  - regenerate the API Server certificate with updated SAN entries.
- The control plane currently consists of a single server node.
- etcd, API Server, Controller Manager, and Scheduler are all managed by systemd.


Part 4 deep dive
# Kubernetes The Hard Way
# Part 4 - Deep Dive: Kubernetes Control Plane Internals


## The Kubernetes Control Plane


The Kubernetes control plane is the "brain" of the cluster. Every Kubernetes operation ultimately passes through it.


```
                kubectl
                   |
                   |
            HTTPS + mTLS
                   |
                   v
          +----------------+
          | kube-apiserver |
          +----------------+
             |     |      |
             |     |      |
             |     |      +---------------------+
             |     |                            |
             |     v                            v
             |  kube-scheduler      kube-controller-manager
             |                            |
             +-------------+--------------+
                           |
                           v
                         etcd
```


---


# kube-apiserver


## Role


The API Server is the single entry point into Kubernetes.


Every component communicates with it:


- kubectl
- kubelet
- kube-proxy
- scheduler
- controller-manager
- external controllers/operators


The API Server:
- authenticates clients,
- authorizes requests,
- runs admission controllers,
- validates objects,
- persists cluster state into etcd.


---


## API Server Startup Sequence


```
systemd
   |
   +--> kube-apiserver.service
            |
            +--> load TLS certificates
            +--> load CA bundle
            +--> load encryption config
            +--> connect to etcd
            +--> initialize RBAC
            +--> initialize admission plugins
            +--> open HTTPS listener (:6443)
            +--> wait for requests
```


---


## Important API Server Flags


### --client-ca-file


Trust bundle for validating client certificates.


Used for mTLS authentication of:
- admin
- kubelet
- scheduler
- controller-manager
- kube-proxy


---


### --tls-cert-file / --tls-private-key-file


Defines the API Server's own TLS identity.


Presented to clients during TLS handshake.


---


### --etcd-servers


Specifies where the API Server stores cluster state.


Every Kubernetes object eventually becomes an etcd entry.


---


### --authorization-mode=Node,RBAC


Authorization pipeline:


```
Authentication
      |
      v
Node Authorizer
      |
      v
RBAC
      |
      v
Allow / Deny
```


---


### Admission Controllers


Request processing:


```
Client
  |
Authentication
  |
Authorization
  |
Admission Controllers
  |
Validation
  |
Store in etcd
```


Enabled admission plugins:
- NamespaceLifecycle
- NodeRestriction
- LimitRanger
- ServiceAccount
- DefaultStorageClass
- ResourceQuota


---


### Encryption Provider Config


The API Server loads `encryption-config.yaml` and encrypts Secret objects before storing them inside etcd.


Flow:


```
Secret YAML
      |
API Server
      |
Read encryption-config.yaml
      |
AES Encrypt
      |
Store ciphertext in etcd
```


---


# kube-controller-manager


## What is it?


The Controller Manager is a collection of independent control loops.


Examples:
- ReplicaSet Controller
- Deployment Controller
- ServiceAccount Controller
- Node Controller
- Endpoint Controller
- CSR Signing Controller


Each controller continuously compares:
- desired state
- actual state


and tries to make them match.


This process is called reconciliation.


---


## Controller Manager as a Client


The Controller Manager is itself a Kubernetes client.


It uses:


```
kube-controller-manager.kubeconfig
```


to:
- connect to API Server,
- authenticate using mTLS,
- read and modify cluster objects.


---


## Cluster Signing


The Controller Manager owns:


- ca.crt
- ca.key


It uses these for signing CSRs submitted by kubelets and other components.


CSR flow:


```
Kubelet
   |
Generate Keypair
   |
Create CSR
   |
API Server
   |
Controller Manager
   |
Uses ca.key
   |
Signs Certificate
   |
Returns Signed Certificate
```


---


## Service Account Tokens


The Controller Manager uses:


```
service-accounts.key
```


to sign JWT tokens that are mounted into Pods.


Pods authenticate to the API Server using these JWTs.


---


# kube-scheduler


## Role


The Scheduler decides which node should run a newly created Pod.


It does NOT start containers.


The Scheduler:
1. watches for Pending Pods,
2. retrieves list of available nodes,
3. scores nodes,
4. chooses the best candidate,
5. updates the Pod's `.spec.nodeName`.


After this, the kubelet notices the assignment and starts the Pod.


---


## Scheduler Workflow


```
User creates Pod
       |
       v
API Server
       |
       v
Pod stored in etcd
       |
       v
Scheduler watches API
       |
       +--> find feasible nodes
       |
       +--> score nodes
       |
       +--> pick winner
       |
       v
Patch Pod:
spec.nodeName=node-1
       |
       v
API Server -> etcd
       |
       v
Kubelet notices assignment
       |
       v
Container starts
```


---


## Scheduler Configuration


The Scheduler uses two files:


### kube-scheduler.service


Managed by systemd.


Purpose:
- launch binary,
- restart policy,
- startup behavior.


### kube-scheduler.yaml


Managed by the Scheduler application.


Contains:
- kubeconfig path,
- leader election settings,
- scheduling plugins,
- scheduler-specific behavior.


---


## Leader Election


Multiple Scheduler instances can run simultaneously.


```
Scheduler-1
Scheduler-2
Scheduler-3
```


Only one becomes the active leader.


```
       Leader
    Scheduler-2
      /      \
     /        \
 standby    standby
```


If the leader crashes, another Scheduler takes over.


---


# RBAC Deep Dive


## ClusterRole


A ClusterRole defines a reusable permission template.


Example:


```
Can:
- read node metrics
- read node logs
- access node proxy


Cannot:
- create deployments
- delete namespaces
```


It does NOT grant permissions by itself.


---


## ClusterRoleBinding


A ClusterRoleBinding assigns a ClusterRole to a user, group, or service account.


In this lab:


```
User: kubernetes
      |
      v
ClusterRoleBinding
      |
      v
ClusterRole:
system:kube-apiserver-to-kubelet
```


---


## Why does the API Server need this?


When executing commands like:


- kubectl logs
- kubectl exec
- kubectl top


the API Server must contact the kubelet running on the worker node.


The kubelet authenticates the API Server using mTLS.


The kubelet then authorizes the API Server based on RBAC.


---


# kube-apiserver-to-kubelet.yaml


This file creates:


1. ClusterRole
2. ClusterRoleBinding


The ClusterRole allows access to:
- nodes/proxy
- nodes/log
- nodes/stats
- nodes/spec
- nodes/metrics


The ClusterRoleBinding grants those permissions to the API Server identity:


```
CN = kubernetes
```


which comes from `kube-api-server.crt`.


---


# systemd vs YAML Configuration


## .service files


Owned by Linux/systemd.


Responsible for:
- starting binaries,
- restart policies,
- boot-time startup,
- passing command-line flags.


Example:


```
ExecStart=/usr/local/bin/kube-apiserver ...
```


---


## Component YAML files


Owned by the Kubernetes application itself.


Responsible for:
- runtime behavior,
- scheduler settings,
- leader election,
- kubeconfig locations.


---


# DNS and Certificate Validation


Connection flow:


```
curl
 |
 |-- /etc/hosts lookup
 |
 |-- TCP connection
 |
 |-- TLS handshake
 |
 |-- Verify CA signature
 |
 |-- Verify SAN matches hostname
 |
 |-- Send HTTP GET /version
 |
 v
API Server
```


---


## Why did `server.ani-kubernetes.local` fail?


The API Server certificate SAN contained:


```
DNS:server.kubernetes.local
```


but not:


```
DNS:server.ani-kubernetes.local
```


TLS hostname validation therefore failed.


The fix was to align `/etc/hosts` with the certificate SAN.


---


# Control Plane Verification Commands


Check service state:


```
systemctl is-active kube-apiserver
systemctl status kube-apiserver
journalctl -u kube-apiserver
```


Verify cluster access:


```
kubectl cluster-info \
  --kubeconfig admin.kubeconfig
```


Verify API endpoint over HTTPS:


```
curl --cacert ca.crt \
  https://server.kubernetes.local:6443/version
```


---


# Big Picture


At the end of Part 4, the following components are operational:


```
                    +------------------+
                    |     kubectl      |
                    +---------+--------+
                              |
                              |
                        HTTPS + mTLS
                              |
                              v
                  +----------------------+
                  |   kube-apiserver     |
                  +-----+----------+-----+
                        |          |
                        |          |
                        v          v
               kube-scheduler   kube-controller-manager
                        \          /
                         \        /
                          \      /
                           \    /
                            \  /
                             \/
                           etcd
```


The control plane is now fully functional and ready for worker nodes to join the cluster.


Part 5 deep dive
# Kubernetes The Hard Way (Part 5)
# Deep Dive: Kubernetes Worker Node Bootstrapping


This document explains in depth the concepts involved while bootstrapping Kubernetes worker nodes.


---


# 1. What is a Worker Node?


A worker node is the machine that actually runs application containers.


A Kubernetes cluster can be viewed as:


                    +----------------------+
                    |    Control Plane     |
                    |----------------------|
                    | kube-apiserver       |
                    | scheduler            |
                    | controller-manager   |
                    | etcd                 |
                    +----------+-----------+
                               |
             -----------------------------------------
             |                                       |
      +------+-------+                       +--------+------+
      |   Worker 1   |                       |   Worker 2    |
      |--------------|                       |---------------|
      | kubelet      |                       | kubelet       |
      | kube-proxy   |                       | kube-proxy    |
      | containerd   |                       | containerd    |
      | CNI Plugins  |                       | CNI Plugins   |
      +--------------+                       +---------------+


The worker's responsibility is to receive Pod assignments from the control plane and execute them.


---


# 2. Major Components


## containerd


containerd is the container runtime.


Responsibilities:
- Pull images.
- Create containers.
- Start/stop containers.
- Manage container lifecycle.


It exposes a gRPC socket:


```
/var/run/containerd/containerd.sock
```


The kubelet communicates with containerd through this socket.


---


## kubelet


kubelet is the Kubernetes node agent.


Responsibilities:
- Authenticate with API server.
- Register node.
- Watch for Pods assigned to this node.
- Ask containerd to run containers.
- Report node status.


The kubelet continuously runs a reconciliation loop:


    Desired State (API Server)
               ↓
          kubelet SyncLoop
               ↓
    Actual State (containerd)


---


## kube-proxy


kube-proxy implements Kubernetes Services.


It watches Service and Endpoint resources from the API server and programs Linux iptables rules.


Without kube-proxy, ClusterIP Services would not work.


---


## CNI Plugins


CNI (Container Network Interface) plugins create networking for Pods.


Responsibilities:
- Create Pod network interfaces.
- Connect Pods to the bridge.
- Allocate Pod IP addresses.


---


# 3. CNI Configuration Files


## 10-bridge.conf


Example:


```json
{
  "name": "bridge",
  "type": "bridge",
  "bridge": "cni0",
  "isGateway": true,
  "ipMasq": true,
  "ipam": {
    "type": "host-local",
    "ranges": [
      [{"subnet": "10.200.1.0/24"}]
    ]
  }
}
```


This creates a Linux bridge called `cni0`.


Each Pod connects to this virtual switch using a veth pair.


```
Pod A --- veth ----+
                   |
                +--+---+
                | cni0 |
                +--+---+
                   |
Pod B --- veth ----+
```


---


## 99-loopback.conf


Provides the loopback interface (`lo`) inside every Pod namespace.


Without it, localhost communication inside Pods would fail.


---


# 4. Why each node gets a different subnet


From `machines.txt`:


```
node-1 10.200.0.0/24
node-2 10.200.1.0/24
```


Each worker receives a unique Pod CIDR.


Example:


- node-1 Pods: 10.200.0.x
- node-2 Pods: 10.200.1.x


This avoids IP collisions.


---


# 5. Why bridge netfilter is required


By default, Linux bridges forward Ethernet frames without sending them through iptables.


```
Pod A --> cni0 --> Pod B
```


iptables never sees the packet.


Kubernetes Services and kube-proxy depend heavily on iptables.


Loading the `br-netfilter` kernel module changes the flow:


```
Pod A
   ↓
cni0 bridge
   ↓
netfilter (iptables)
   ↓
Destination Pod
```


Commands used:


```
modprobe br-netfilter
```


Loads the kernel module.


```
echo "br-netfilter" >> /etc/modules-load.d/modules.conf
```


Ensures the module loads after reboot.


```
net.bridge.bridge-nf-call-iptables = 1
```


Instructs the kernel to pass bridge traffic through iptables.


---


# 6. Why sysctl is used


Files under `/etc/sysctl.d/` store kernel runtime parameters.


```
sysctl -p /etc/sysctl.d/kubernetes.conf
```


Immediately applies those values without requiring a reboot.


Internally, it writes values under:


```
/proc/sys/net/bridge/
```


---


# 7. Why both .service and .yaml files exist


## YAML files


YAML files configure the application itself.


Example:


- kubelet-config.yaml
- kube-proxy-config.yaml


These contain component-specific settings:
- API endpoints.
- Authentication.
- Runtime socket paths.
- Cluster CIDRs.
- TLS file locations.


Example:


```yaml
clientConnection:
  kubeconfig: "/var/lib/kube-proxy/kubeconfig"
```


This tells kube-proxy how to connect to the API server.


---


## systemd service files


Service files tell Linux how to start the application.


Example:


```ini
ExecStart=/usr/local/bin/kubelet \
  --config=/var/lib/kubelet/kubelet-config.yaml \
  --kubeconfig=/var/lib/kubelet/kubeconfig
```


The `.service` file says:
- Which executable to run.
- Which YAML config to load.
- Restart policy.
- Startup dependencies.


Think of it as:


- `.service` = "How do I launch the process?"
- `.yaml` = "How should the process behave after launching?"


---


# 8. Kubelet Registration Flow


When kubelet starts:


1. Reads `kubelet.service`.
2. Reads `kubelet-config.yaml`.
3. Reads `/var/lib/kubelet/kubeconfig`.
4. Resolves `server.kubernetes.local`.
5. Performs mTLS handshake.
6. Authenticates using `kubelet.crt` and `kubelet.key`.
7. Sends:


```
POST /api/v1/nodes
```


8. API server stores Node object in etcd.
9. Kubelet starts watching API resources.
10. Node becomes `Ready`.


---


# 9. Understanding the kubelet logs


## "Attempting to register node"


The kubelet is sending a registration request to the API server.


## "Successfully registered node"


The API server accepted the request and created the Node object.


## "NodeReady"


The kubelet has confirmed:
- sufficient memory,
- no disk pressure,
- sufficient PIDs,
- runtime available,
- networking initialized.


## "Caches populated"


The kubelet's internal client-go Informers have synchronized with the API server.


Instead of polling constantly, kubelet opens long-lived watch connections.


---


# 10. Why the /etc/hosts fix was necessary


The kubelet kubeconfig contains:


```
server: https://server.kubernetes.local:6443
```


The worker node must resolve this hostname.


Initially:


```
lookup server.kubernetes.local:
Temporary failure in name resolution
```


Adding:


```
172.31.20.244 server.kubernetes.local server
```


to `/etc/hosts` solved the issue.


---


# 11. Final Worker Architecture


                    +-----------------------------+
                    |      kube-apiserver         |
                    |   https://server:6443       |
                    +--------------+--------------+
                                   ^
                                   |
                            mTLS via kubeconfig
                                   |
             +---------------------+---------------------+
             |                                           |
    +--------+---------+                        +---------+--------+
    |      kubelet     |                        |      kubelet     |
    |      node-1      |                        |      node-2      |
    +--------+---------+                        +---------+--------+
             |                                            |
             | CRI gRPC                                   | CRI gRPC
             v                                            v
      +--------------+                            +---------------+
      |  containerd  |                            |  containerd   |
      +------+-------+                            +-------+-------+
             |                                            |
             | CNI                                        | CNI
             v                                            v
         +-------+                                    +-------+
         | cni0  |                                    | cni0  |
         +-------+                                    +-------+


Meanwhile:


- kube-proxy watches Services and Endpoints.
- containerd manages containers.
- CNI manages Pod networking.
- kubelet reconciles desired state with actual state.


When `kubectl get nodes` shows:


```
NAME     STATUS   ROLES    AGE   VERSION
node-1   Ready    <none>   ...
node-2   Ready    <none>   ...
```


it means this entire architecture is functioning correctly.


Part 5 implementation
# Kubernetes The Hard Way on AWS (Part 5)
# Bootstrapping the Kubernetes Worker Nodes


This document covers the implementation steps performed while bootstrapping the Kubernetes worker nodes (`node-1` and `node-2`) on AWS EC2 instances. The commands below reflect the modifications required for our AWS-based setup.


---


# 1. Copy Worker Configuration Files


From the jumpbox, generate worker-specific CNI and kubelet configuration files.


```bash
for HOST in node-1 node-2; do
  SUBNET=$(grep ${HOST} machines.txt | cut -d " " -f 4)


  sed "s|SUBNET|$SUBNET|g" \
    configs/10-bridge.conf > 10-bridge.conf


  sed "s|SUBNET|$SUBNET|g" \
    configs/kubelet-config.yaml > kubelet-config.yaml


  scp 10-bridge.conf kubelet-config.yaml \
    ${HOST}:~/
done
```


This dynamically injects the Pod CIDR allocated to each worker.


---


# 2. Copy Worker Binaries and Service Files


```bash
for HOST in node-1 node-2; do
  scp \
    downloads/worker/* \
    downloads/client/kubectl \
    configs/99-loopback.conf \
    configs/containerd-config.toml \
    configs/kube-proxy-config.yaml \
    units/containerd.service \
    units/kubelet.service \
    units/kube-proxy.service \
    ${HOST}:~/
done
```


---


# 3. Copy CNI Plugins


```bash
for HOST in node-1 node-2; do
  ssh ${HOST} "mkdir -p ~/cni-plugins"


  scp downloads/cni-plugins/* \
    ${HOST}:~/cni-plugins/
done
```


---


# 4. Offline Installation of Required Packages


Since worker nodes did not have internet access, packages were downloaded on the jumpbox.


## Download packages


```bash
mkdir ~/worker-debs
cd ~/worker-debs


apt download socat conntrack ipset kmod
apt download libipset13t64
```


## Copy packages to worker nodes


```bash
scp *.deb node-1:~/
scp *.deb node-2:~/
```


## Install packages


On each worker:


```bash
sudo dpkg -i *.deb
```


If ipset dependency fails:


```bash
sudo dpkg -i libipset13t64*.deb
sudo dpkg -i ipset*.deb
```


Installed packages:
- socat
- conntrack
- ipset
- kmod
- libipset13t64


---


# 5. Verify Swap


```bash
swapon --show
```


Output should be empty. If not:


```bash
sudo swapoff -a
```


---


# 6. Create Required Directories


```bash
sudo mkdir -p \
  /etc/cni/net.d \
  /opt/cni/bin \
  /var/lib/kubelet \
  /var/lib/kube-proxy \
  /var/lib/kubernetes \
  /var/run/kubernetes
```


---


# 7. Install Worker Binaries


```bash
sudo mv crictl kube-proxy kubelet runc \
  /usr/local/bin/


sudo mv containerd containerd-shim-runc-v2 \
  containerd-stress /bin/


sudo mv cni-plugins/* /opt/cni/bin/
```


---


# 8. Configure CNI Networking


Move configuration files:


```bash
sudo mv 10-bridge.conf 99-loopback.conf \
  /etc/cni/net.d/
```


---


# 9. Enable Bridge Netfilter


Load kernel module:


```bash
sudo modprobe br-netfilter
```


Persist after reboot:


```bash
echo "br-netfilter" | \
sudo tee -a /etc/modules-load.d/modules.conf
```


Enable bridge traffic processing through iptables:


```bash
echo "net.bridge.bridge-nf-call-iptables = 1" | \
sudo tee -a /etc/sysctl.d/kubernetes.conf


echo "net.bridge.bridge-nf-call-ip6tables = 1" | \
sudo tee -a /etc/sysctl.d/kubernetes.conf


sudo sysctl -p /etc/sysctl.d/kubernetes.conf
```


---


# 10. Configure containerd


```bash
sudo mkdir -p /etc/containerd


sudo mv containerd-config.toml \
  /etc/containerd/config.toml


sudo mv containerd.service \
  /etc/systemd/system/
```


---


# 11. Configure kubelet


```bash
sudo mv kubelet-config.yaml \
  /var/lib/kubelet/


sudo mv kubelet.service \
  /etc/systemd/system/
```


---


# 12. Configure kube-proxy


```bash
sudo mv kube-proxy-config.yaml \
  /var/lib/kube-proxy/


sudo mv kube-proxy.service \
  /etc/systemd/system/
```


---


# 13. Start Worker Services


```bash
sudo systemctl daemon-reload


sudo systemctl enable \
  containerd kubelet kube-proxy


sudo systemctl start \
  containerd kubelet kube-proxy
```


Verify:


```bash
sudo systemctl is-active kubelet
```


Expected output:


```
active
```


---


# 14. Fix Worker Host Resolution


Add cluster entries to `/etc/hosts` on both worker nodes:


```text
172.31.20.244 server.kubernetes.local server
172.31.19.88 node-1.ani-kubernetes.local node-1
172.31.25.215 node-2.ani-kubernetes.local node-2
```


This is necessary because kubelet connects to:


```
https://server.kubernetes.local:6443
```


---


# 15. Verify Worker Registration


From the jumpbox:


```bash
ssh admin@server \
  "kubectl get nodes \
  --kubeconfig admin.kubeconfig"
```


Expected output:


```text
NAME     STATUS   ROLES    AGE     VERSION
node-1   Ready    <none>   4m8s    v1.32.3
node-2   Ready    <none>   2m15s   v1.32.3
```


At this stage, both worker nodes have successfully joined the Kubernetes cluster.


Part 6 implementstion
# Kubernetes The Hard Way (AWS Edition)
# Part 6 - Smoke Test and Cluster Validation


## Objective


The purpose of this section is to verify that all major Kubernetes control plane and worker node components are functioning correctly after the cluster has been bootstrapped.


The smoke test validates:
- Secret encryption at rest.
- API Server ↔ etcd integration.
- Deployment and ReplicaSet controllers.
- Scheduler functionality.
- Kubelet and container runtime.
- Pod networking.
- kubectl port forwarding.
- Container log retrieval.
- Container exec functionality.
- Service creation and kube-proxy operation.


---


## 1. Verify Secret Encryption


Create a Kubernetes Secret:


```bash
kubectl create secret generic kubernetes-the-hard-way \
  --from-literal="mykey=mydata"
```


Verify how the secret is stored inside etcd:


```bash
ssh admin@server \
  "sudo etcdctl get /registry/secrets/default/kubernetes-the-hard-way | hexdump -C"
```


Expected:
- The output should not contain plain text.
- The stored value should begin with:


```
k8s:enc:aescbc:v1:key1
```


This confirms that the encryption configuration (`encryption-config.yaml`) generated earlier is working.


---


## 2. Create an NGINX Deployment


### Offline Image Import


Since the worker nodes do not have Internet access, container images were manually loaded.


### On the jumpbox:


Install Docker (if not already installed):


```bash
sudo apt update
sudo apt install -y docker.io
sudo systemctl enable --now docker
```


Pull the required images:


```bash
docker pull nginx:latest
docker pull registry.k8s.io/pause:3.10
```


Export them:


```bash
docker save -o nginx.tar nginx:latest
docker save -o pause.tar registry.k8s.io/pause:3.10
```


Copy the images and the `ctr` binary to both workers:


```bash
scp nginx.tar pause.tar ctr admin@node-1:~/
scp nginx.tar pause.tar ctr admin@node-2:~/
```


On each worker:


```bash
chmod +x ~/ctr
sudo mv ~/ctr /usr/local/bin/


sudo ctr -n k8s.io images import nginx.tar
sudo ctr -n k8s.io images import pause.tar
```


Verify:


```bash
sudo ctr -n k8s.io images ls
```


Expected images:
- docker.io/library/nginx:latest
- registry.k8s.io/pause:3.10


---


## 3. Create Deployment


Generate deployment YAML:


```bash
kubectl create deployment nginx \
  --image=nginx:latest \
  --dry-run=client -o yaml > nginx.yaml
```


Modify the container specification:


```yaml
containers:
- image: nginx:latest
  imagePullPolicy: IfNotPresent
  name: nginx
```


Apply the deployment:


```bash
kubectl create -f nginx.yaml
```


Verify:


```bash
kubectl get pods
```


Expected:


```
NAME                     READY   STATUS    RESTARTS   AGE
nginx-xxxxxxxxxx-xxxxx   1/1     Running   0          XXs
```


---


## 4. Port Forwarding Test


Retrieve the pod name:


```bash
POD_NAME=$(kubectl get pods -l app=nginx \
  -o jsonpath="{.items[0].metadata.name}")
```


Forward local port 8080 to port 80 inside the Pod:


```bash
kubectl port-forward $POD_NAME 8080:80
```


In another terminal:


```bash
curl --head http://127.0.0.1:8080
```


Expected:


```
HTTP/1.1 200 OK
Server: nginx/1.31.1
```


Stop port forwarding with:


```
Ctrl+C
```


---


## 5. Container Logs


Retrieve container logs:


```bash
kubectl logs $POD_NAME
```


Expected:
- NGINX startup logs.
- HTTP access logs generated by the curl request.


---


## 6. Execute Commands Inside Container


Verify remote execution:


```bash
kubectl exec -ti $POD_NAME -- nginx -v
```


Expected:


```
nginx version: nginx/1.31.1
```


---


## 7. Create a NodePort Service


Expose the Deployment:


```bash
kubectl expose deployment nginx \
  --port 80 --type NodePort
```


Retrieve the assigned NodePort:


```bash
NODE_PORT=$(kubectl get svc nginx \
  --output=jsonpath='{.spec.ports[0].nodePort}')
```


Retrieve the node hosting the Pod:


```bash
NODE_NAME=$(kubectl get pods \
  -l app=nginx \
  -o jsonpath="{.items[0].spec.nodeName}")
```


Verify access:


```bash
curl -I http://${NODE_NAME}:${NODE_PORT}
```


Expected:


```
HTTP/1.1 200 OK
Server: nginx/1.31.1
```


---


## Issues Encountered During AWS Deployment


### 1. Worker Nodes Without Public IP


The worker nodes could not pull images directly from Docker Hub or `registry.k8s.io`.


Solution:
- Pull images on the jumpbox.
- Save them using `docker save`.
- Copy via `scp`.
- Import using `ctr images import`.


### 2. Missing `pause` Image


Initial Pod creation failed with:


```
FailedCreatePodSandbox
failed to pull registry.k8s.io/pause:3.10
```


The `pause` image was manually imported into both workers.


### 3. Deployment Controller Failure


Initially, Deployments were created but no Pods appeared.


Root cause:
- `kube-controller-manager` could not resolve `server.kubernetes.local`.
- `/etc/hosts` was updated on the controller node.
- Controller Manager and Scheduler were restarted.


After fixing DNS, Deployments, ReplicaSets, and Pods were created correctly.


---

|-----------|---------|
