The Kubernetes cluster is now fully operational.


Part 6 deep dive
# Kubernetes The Hard Way
# Part 6 - Deep Dive into the Smoke Test


## Purpose of the Smoke Test


The Smoke Test is not simply a functional test. It is an end-to-end validation of almost every major Kubernetes subsystem.


A single successful `kubectl create deployment nginx` exercises:


- kubectl
- kubeconfig authentication
- mTLS
- API Server
- etcd
- Controller Manager
- ReplicaSet Controller
- Scheduler
- Kubelet
- containerd
- CNI plugins
- kube-proxy
- Linux namespaces
- Pod networking


---


# 1. Secret Encryption at Rest


When running:


```bash
kubectl create secret generic kubernetes-the-hard-way \
  --from-literal="mykey=mydata"
```


the following occurs:


```
kubectl
   │
   ▼
API Server
   │
   ├── Authenticates user using client certificate
   │
   ├── Applies admission controllers
   │
   ├── Detects object type = Secret
   │
   ├── Reads encryption-config.yaml
   │
   ├── Encrypts payload using AES-CBC
   │
   ▼
etcd
```


The encryption provider adds a prefix:


```
k8s:enc:aescbc:v1:key1
```


This prefix records:
- Encryption mechanism (`aescbc`)
- Provider version (`v1`)
- Encryption key identifier (`key1`)


The API Server is the only component that performs encryption and decryption. etcd simply stores opaque bytes.


---


# 2. Kubernetes Deployments


A Deployment is a high-level declarative object.


You never directly create Pods. Instead:


```
Deployment
      │
      ▼
ReplicaSet
      │
      ▼
Pod
```


Deployment responsibilities:
- Desired replica count.
- Rolling updates.
- Rollbacks.
- ReplicaSet management.


ReplicaSet responsibilities:
- Ensure the desired number of Pods exists.


---


# 3. Kubernetes Reconciliation Loop


Kubernetes is built around reconciliation loops.


You tell Kubernetes:


```
I want one nginx Pod.
```


You never say:


```
Create a Pod now.
```


Instead:


```
Desired State
      │
      ▼
Stored in etcd
      │
      ▼
Controller Manager watches API Server
      │
      ▼
Creates ReplicaSet
      │
      ▼
ReplicaSet creates Pod object
      │
      ▼
Scheduler assigns node
      │
      ▼
Kubelet creates container
```


The Controller Manager continuously compares:
- Desired State
- Actual State


and tries to make them equal.


---


# 4. The Scheduler


The scheduler does not create Pods.


It only decides where a Pod should run.


Input:
- Unscheduled Pod object.


Output:
- Adds:


```yaml
spec:
  nodeName: node-2
```


to the Pod object.


The Kubelet on node-2 watches for Pods assigned to it and begins execution.


---


# 5. Pod Sandbox and the Pause Container


This is one of Kubernetes' most important internal concepts.


## Why a Pause Container Exists


A Pod may contain multiple containers:


```
Pod
 ├── nginx
 ├── sidecar
 └── metrics exporter
```


All containers must share:
- Network namespace.
- localhost.
- IPC namespace.
- (Optionally) PID namespace.


Linux namespaces must belong to a running process.


Therefore Kubernetes creates a tiny "pause" container whose only job is to own these namespaces.


```
Pod
 ├── pause:3.10
 │      ├── Network namespace
 │      ├── IPC namespace
 │      └── PID namespace (optional)
 │
 ├── nginx
 └── sidecar
```


The pause process is essentially:


```c
int main() {
    while (1)
        pause();
}
```


It sleeps forever.


---


# 6. Pod Creation Sequence


Creating a Pod actually involves multiple phases:


```
1. Deployment created.
2. ReplicaSet created.
3. Pod object created.
4. Scheduler binds Pod to node.
5. Kubelet observes assignment.
6. Kubelet requests PodSandbox creation.
7. containerd starts pause container.
8. CNI plugin attaches network.
9. Pod receives IP address.
10. Application containers start.
11. Pod enters Running state.
```


Your cluster initially failed at Step 7 because the `pause:3.10` image could not be downloaded.


---


# 7. Container Runtime (containerd)


Kubelet never starts containers directly.


Instead:


```
Kubelet
    │
    ▼
CRI (Container Runtime Interface)
    │
    ▼
containerd
    │
    ▼
runc
    │
    ▼
Linux namespaces + cgroups
```


Containerd handles:
- Pulling images.
- Creating sandboxes.
- Managing container lifecycle.
- Calling `runc`.


---


# 8. Port Forwarding


Command:


```bash
kubectl port-forward POD 8080:80
```


Flow:


```
curl localhost:8080
        │
        ▼
kubectl
        │
(mTLS)
        ▼
API Server
        │
SPDY/WebSocket stream
        ▼
Kubelet
        │
        ▼
Container port 80
```


No Service or kube-proxy is required.


Port forwarding tunnels traffic directly through the API Server.


---


# 9. Retrieving Logs


When running:


```bash
kubectl logs POD_NAME
```


Flow:


```
kubectl
   │
   ▼
API Server
   │
   ▼
Kubelet
   │
   ▼
containerd
   │
   ▼
Container stdout/stderr
```


The API Server simply proxies the request.


---


# 10. Executing Commands Inside Containers


Command:


```bash
kubectl exec -ti POD -- nginx -v
```


Flow:


```
kubectl
   │
   ▼
API Server
   │
   ▼
Kubelet
   │
   ▼
containerd CRI Exec API
   │
   ▼
runc exec
   │
   ▼
New process created inside existing container namespaces
```


The original container is not restarted. A new process joins the already-running namespace.


---


# 11. NodePort Services and kube-proxy


A Service is a stable virtual endpoint in front of Pods.


```
Client
   │
   ▼
NodePort
   │
   ▼
kube-proxy (iptables rules)
   │
   ▼
Pod IP
   │
   ▼
nginx container
```


The Service itself does not forward packets.


kube-proxy watches the API Server and programs Linux `iptables` rules.


Packets entering the NodePort are transparently rewritten and forwarded to the correct Pod.


---


# 12. Why the Entire Smoke Test Matters


A successful smoke test proves that the following chain is operational:


```
kubectl
   │
   ▼
Client Certificate Authentication
   │
   ▼
API Server
   │
   ▼
etcd
   │
   ▼
Controller Manager
   │
   ▼
ReplicaSet Controller
   │
   ▼
Scheduler
   │
   ▼
Kubelet
   │
   ▼
containerd
   │
   ▼
Pause Container
   │
   ▼
Application Container
   │
   ▼
CNI Networking
   │
   ▼
kube-proxy
   │
   ▼
User Traffic
```


A successful `kubectl get pods`, `port-forward`, `logs`, `exec`, and `NodePort` test together validate almost every major Kubernetes component that was manually bootstrapped throughout the Kubernetes The Hard Way journey.
